<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Mind</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Faculties responsible for mental phenomena</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Mind_(disambiguation)" title="Mind (disambiguation)">Mind (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phrenology1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="529" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Phrenology1.jpg/220px-Phrenology1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Phrenology1.jpg/330px-Phrenology1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Phrenology1.jpg/440px-Phrenology1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phrenology1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Phrenology" title="Phrenology">phrenological</a> <a href="/wiki/Brain_mapping" title="Brain mapping">mapping</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> of the brain. Phrenology was among the first attempts to correlate mental functions with specific parts of the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>mind</b> is the set of faculties responsible for all mental <a href="/wiki/Phenomenon" title="Phenomenon">phenomena</a>. Often the term is also identified with the phenomena themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaMind_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaMind-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> These faculties include <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thought</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">imagination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Will_(philosophy)" title="Will (philosophy)">will</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensation_(psychology)" title="Sensation (psychology)">sensation</a>. They are responsible for various mental phenomena, like <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain experience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desire" title="Desire">desire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intention</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotion</a>. Various overlapping classifications of mental phenomena have been proposed. Important distinctions group them together according to whether they are <i>sensory</i>, <i>propositional</i>, <i>intentional</i>, <i>conscious</i>, or <i>occurrent</i>. Minds were traditionally understood as <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substances</a> but it is more common in the contemporary perspective to conceive them as <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a> or capacities possessed by humans and higher animals. Various competing definitions of the exact nature of the mind or mentality have been proposed. <i>Epistemic definitions</i> focus on the privileged epistemic access the subject has to these states. <i>Consciousness-based approaches</i> give primacy to the conscious mind and allow unconscious mental phenomena as part of the mind only to the extent that they stand in the right relation to the conscious mind. According to <i>intentionality-based approaches</i>, the power to refer to objects and to represent the <a href="/wiki/World#Philosophy_of_mind" title="World">world</a> is the mark of the mental. For <i>behaviorism</i>, whether an entity has a mind only depends on how it behaves in response to external stimuli while <i>functionalism</i> defines mental states in terms of the causal roles they play. Central questions for the study of mind, like whether other entities besides humans have minds or how the relation between body and mind is to be conceived, are strongly influenced by the choice of one's definition.
</p><p>Mind or mentality is usually contrasted with body, matter or physicality. The issue of the nature of this contrast and specifically the relation between mind and brain is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind-body_problem" title="Mind-body problem">mind-body problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">[5]</a></sup> Traditional viewpoints included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">dualism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a>, which consider the mind to be non-physical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5">[5]</a></sup> Modern views often center around <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Functionalism (philosophy of mind)">functionalism</a>, which hold that the mind is roughly identical with the brain or <a href="/wiki/Reductionism" title="Reductionism">reducible</a> to physical phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuronal activity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (December 2016)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup> though dualism and idealism continue to have many supporters. Another question concerns which types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">beings</a> are capable of having minds.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> For example, whether mind is exclusive to humans, possessed also by some or all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animals" title="Animals">animals</a>, by all <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">living things</a>, whether it is a strictly definable characteristic at all, or whether mind can also be a property of some types of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">human-made machines</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Different cultural and religious traditions often use different concepts of mind, resulting in different answers to these questions. Some see mind as a property exclusive to humans whereas others ascribe properties of mind to non-living entities (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Panpsychism" title="Panpsychism">panpsychism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a>), to animals and to <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>. Some of the earliest recorded speculations linked mind (sometimes described as identical with <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">spirit</a>) to theories concerning both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_after_death" title="Life after death">life after death</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nature_(philosophy)" title="Nature (philosophy)">natural</a> order, for example in the doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">the Buddha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, and other ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian</a> and, later, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_psychological_thought" title="Islamic psychological thought">Islamic</a> and medieval European philosophers.
</p><p>Psychologists such as <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Freud</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">James</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer scientists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Turing</a> developed influential theories about the nature of the mind. The possibility of nonbiological minds is explored in the field of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, which works closely in relation with <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a> to understand the ways in which information processing by nonbiological machines is comparable or different to mental phenomena in the human mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The mind is also sometimes portrayed as the <a href="/wiki/Stream_of_consciousness_(psychology)" title="Stream of consciousness (psychology)">stream of consciousness</a> where sense impressions and mental phenomena are constantly changing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karunamuni2017_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karunamuni2017-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aggregates_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aggregates-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The original meaning of <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gemynd" title="wikt:gemynd">gemynd</a></i> was the faculty of <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>, not of thought in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Hence <i>call to mind</i>, <i>come to mind</i>, <i>keep in mind</i>, <i>to have mind of</i>, etc. The word retains this sense in Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> had other words to express "mind", such as <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hyge" title="wikt:hyge">hyge</a></i> "mind, spirit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The meaning of "memory" is shared with <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>, which has <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muninn" title="Muninn">munr</a></i>. The word is originally from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PIE" title="PIE">PIE</a> verbal root <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">*men-</span></span></i>, meaning "to think, remember", whence also Latin<i> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mens#Latin" title="wikt:mens">mens</a></i> "mind", Sanskrit <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E0%A4%AE%E0%A4%A8%E0%A4%B8%E0%A5%8D" title="wikt:मनस्"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">manas</i></span></a></i> "mind" and Greek <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BC%CE%AD%CE%BD%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:μένος">μένος</a> "mind, courage, anger".
</p><p>The generalization of <i>mind</i> to include all mental faculties, thought, <a href="/wiki/Volition_(psychology)" title="Volition (psychology)">volition</a>, feeling and memory, gradually develops over the 14th and 15th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The mind is often understood as a faculty that manifests itself in <a href="/wiki/Mental_state" title="Mental state">mental phenomena</a> like sensation, perception, thinking, reasoning, memory, belief, desire, emotion and motivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaMind_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaMind-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup> Mind or mentality is usually contrasted with body, matter or physicality. Central to this contrast is the <a href="/wiki/Intuition" title="Intuition">intuition</a> that minds exhibit various features not found in and maybe even incompatible with the material universe as described by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_sciences" title="Natural sciences">natural sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup> On the traditionally dominant substantialist view associated with <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, minds are defined as independent thinking substances. But it is more common in contemporary philosophy to conceive minds not as <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substances</a> but as <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a> or capacities possessed by humans and higher animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichMind_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichMind-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite this agreement, there is still a lot of difference of opinion concerning what the exact nature of mind is and various competing definitions have been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaMind_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaMind-2">[2]</a></sup> Philosophical definitions of mind usually proceed not just by listing various types of phenomena belonging to the mind but by searching the "mark of the mental": a feature that is shared by all mental states and only by mental states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup> <i>Epistemic approaches</i> define mental states in terms of the privileged epistemic access the subject has to these states. This is often combined with a <i>consciousness-based approach</i>, which emphasizes the primacy of consciousness in relation to mind. <i>Intentionality-based approaches</i>, on the other hand, see the power of minds to refer to objects and represent the <a href="/wiki/World#Philosophy_of_mind" title="World">world</a> as being a certain way as the <i>mark of the mental</i>. According to <i>behaviorism</i>, whether an entity has a mind only depends on how it behaves in response to external stimuli while <i>functionalism</i> defines mental states in terms of the causal roles they play. The differences between these diverse approaches are substantial since they result in very different answers to questions like whether animals or computers have minds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaMind_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaMind-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a great variety of mental states. They fall into categories like sensory and non-sensory or conscious and unconscious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup> Various of the definitions listed above excel for states from one category but struggle to account for why states from another category are also part of the mind. This has led some theorists to doubt that there is a <i>mark of the mental</i>. So maybe the term "mind" just refers to a cluster of loosely related ideas that do not share one unifying feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichMind_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichMind-17">[17]</a></sup> Some theorists have responded to this by narrowing their definitions of mind to "higher" intellectual faculties, like thinking, reasoning and memory. Others try to be as inclusive as possible regarding "lower" intellectual faculties, like sensing and emotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In popular usage, <i>mind</i> is frequently synonymous with <i>thought</i>: the private conversation with ourselves that we carry on "inside our heads".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Thus we "make up our minds", "change our minds" or are "of two minds" about something. One of the key attributes of the mind in this sense is that it is a private sphere to which no one but the owner has access. No one else can "know our mind". They can only interpret what we consciously or unconsciously communicate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemic_and_consciousness-based_approaches">Epistemic and consciousness-based approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Epistemic and consciousness-based approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistemic" title="Epistemic">Epistemic</a> approaches</i> emphasize that the subject has privileged access to all or at least some of their mental states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaPhilosophy_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaPhilosophy-22">[22]</a></sup> It is sometimes claimed that this access is <i>direct</i>, <i>private</i> and <i>infallible</i>. <i>Direct access</i> refers to non-inferential knowledge. When someone is in pain, for example, they know directly that they are in pain, they do not need to infer it from other indicators like a body part being swollen or their tendency to scream when it is touched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup> But we arguably also have non-inferential knowledge of external objects, like trees or cats, through perception, which is why this criterion by itself is not sufficient. Another epistemic privilege often mentioned is that mental states are <i>private</i> in contrast to <i>public</i> external facts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-9"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaPhilosophy_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaPhilosophy-22">[22]</a></sup> For example, the fallen tree lying on a person's leg is directly open to perception by the bystanders while the victim's pain is private: only they know it directly while the bystanders have to infer it from their screams. It was traditionally often claimed that we have infallible knowledge of our own mental states, i.e. that we cannot be wrong about them when we have them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-10"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup> So when someone has an itching sensation, for example, they cannot be wrong about having this sensation. They can only be wrong about the non-mental causes, e.g. whether it is the consequence of bug bites or of a fungal infection. But various counterexamples have been presented to claims of infallibility, which is why this criterion is usually not accepted in contemporary philosophy. One problem for all <i>epistemic approaches</i> to the <i>mark of the mental</i> is that they focus mainly on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conscious" title="Conscious">conscious</a> states but exclude <a href="/wiki/Unconscious_mind" title="Unconscious mind">unconscious</a> states. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Repression_(psychology)" title="Repression (psychology)">repressed</a> <a href="/wiki/Desire" title="Desire">desire</a>, for example, is a mental state to which the subject lacks the forms of privileged epistemic access mentioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-11"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichMind_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichMind-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>One way to respond to this worry is to ascribe a privileged status to conscious mental states. On such a <i>consciousness-based approach</i>, conscious mental states are non-derivative constituents of the mind while unconscious states somehow depend on their conscious counterparts for their existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaPhilosophy_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaPhilosophy-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bourget_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bourget-23">[23]</a></sup> An influential example of this position is due to <a href="/wiki/John_Searle" title="John Searle">John Searle</a>, who holds that unconscious mental states have to be accessible to consciousness to count as "mental" at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> They can be understood as dispositions to bring about conscious states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This position denies that the so-called "deep unconscious", i.e. mental contents inaccessible to consciousness, exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillett_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gillett-26">[26]</a></sup> Another problem for <i>consciousness-based approaches</i>, besides the issue of accounting for the unconscious mind, is to elucidate the nature of consciousness itself.  Consciousness-based approaches are usually interested in <i><a href="/wiki/Consciousness#Types_of_consciousness" title="Consciousness">phenomenal consciousness</a></i>, i.e. in qualitative experience, rather than <i><a href="/wiki/Consciousness#Types_of_consciousness" title="Consciousness">access consciousness</a></i>, which refers to information being available for reasoning and guiding behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard2018_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard2018-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Conscious mental states are normally characterized as qualitative and subjective, i.e. that there is something it is like for a subject to be in these states. Opponents of consciousness-based approaches often point out that despite these attempts, it is still very unclear what the term "phenomenal consciousness" is supposed to mean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup> This is important because not much would be gained theoretically by defining one ill-understood term in terms of another. Another objection to this type of approach is to deny that the conscious mind has a privileged status in relation to the unconscious mind, for example, by insisting that the deep unconscious exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bourget_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bourget-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillett_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gillett-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intentionality-based_approaches">Intentionality-based approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Intentionality-based approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Intentionality-based approaches</i> see <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a> as the <i>mark of the mental</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-12"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup> The originator of this approach is <a href="/wiki/Franz_Brentano" title="Franz Brentano">Franz Brentano</a>, who defined intentionality as the characteristic of mental states to refer to or be about objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franz_Brentano:_3.2_Intentionality_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Franz_Brentano:_3.2_Intentionality-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crane1998_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crane1998-30">[30]</a></sup> One central idea for this approach is that minds represent the world around them, which is not the case for regular physical objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> So a person who believes that there is ice cream in the fridge represents the world as being a certain way. The ice cream can be represented but it does not itself represent the world. This is why a mind is ascribed to the person but not to the ice cream, according to the intentional approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-13"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup> One advantage of it in comparison to the <i>epistemic approach</i> is that it has no problems to account for unconscious mental states: they can be intentional just like conscious mental states and thereby qualify as constituents of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siewert_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siewert-32">[32]</a></sup> But a problem for this approach is that there are also some non-mental entities that have intentionality, like maps or linguistic expressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2006_15-14"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2006-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacob_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacob-33">[33]</a></sup> One response to this problem is to hold that the intentionality of non-mental entities is somehow derivative in relation to the intentionality of mental entities. For example, a map of Addis Ababa may be said to represent Addis Ababa not intrinsically but only extrinsically because people <i>interpret</i> it as a representation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siewert_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Siewert-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Another difficulty is that not all mental states seem to be intentional. So while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beliefs" title="Beliefs">beliefs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desires" title="Desires">desires</a> are forms of representation, this seems not to be the case for pains and itches, which may <i>indicate</i> a problem without <i>representing</i> it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crane1998_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crane1998-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacob_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacob-33">[33]</a></sup> But some theorists have argued that even these apparent counterexamples should be considered intentional when properly understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviorism_and_functionalism">Behaviorism and functionalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Behaviorism and functionalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Behaviorism" title="Behaviorism">Behaviorist</a></i> definitions characterize mental states as dispositions to engage in certain publicly observable behavior as a reaction to particular external stimuli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-38">[38]</a></sup> On this view, to ascribe a belief to someone is to describe the tendency of this person to behave in certain ways. Such an ascription does not involve any claims about the internal states of this person, it only talks about behavioral tendencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-38">[38]</a></sup> A strong motivation for such a position comes from empiricist considerations stressing the importance of observation and the lack thereof in the case of private internal mental states. This is sometimes combined with the thesis that we could not even learn how to use mental terms without reference to the behavior associated with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-38">[38]</a></sup> One problem for behaviorism is that the same entity often behaves differently despite being in the same situation as before. This suggests that explanation needs to make reference to the internal states of the entity that mediate the link between stimulus and response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This problem is avoided by <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Functionalism (philosophy of mind)">functionalist</a> approaches, which define mental states through their causal roles but allow both external and internal events in their causal network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polger_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polger-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichMind_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichMind-17">[17]</a></sup> On this view, the definition of pain-state may include aspects such as being in a state that "tends to be caused by bodily injury, to produce the belief that something is wrong with the body and ... to cause wincing or moaning".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>One important aspect of both behaviorist and functionalist approaches is that, according to them, the mind is <i><a href="/wiki/Multiple_realizability" title="Multiple realizability">multiply realizable</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> This means that it does not depend on the exact constitution of an entity for whether it has a mind or not. Instead, only its behavioral dispositions or its role in the causal network matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polger_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polger-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-43">[43]</a></sup> The entity in question may be a human, an animal, a silicon-based alien or a robot. Functionalists sometimes draw an analogy to the software-hardware distinction where the mind is likened to a certain type of software that can be installed on different forms of hardware. Closely linked to this analogy is the thesis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computationalism" title="Computationalism">computationalism</a>, which defines the mind as an information processing system that is physically implemented by the neural activity of the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>One problem for all of these views is that they seem to be unable to account for the phenomenal consciousness of the mind emphasized by <i>consciousness-based approaches</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup> It may be true that pains are caused by bodily injuries and themselves produce certain beliefs and moaning behavior. But the causal profile of pain remains silent on the intrinsic unpleasantness of the painful experience itself. Some states that are not painful to the subject at all may even fit these characterizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms">Forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mental_faculties">Mental faculties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mental faculties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
