Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Mind%22" rel="nofollow">"Mind"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Mind%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Mind%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Mind%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Mind%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Mind%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Broadly speaking, mental faculties are the various functions of the mind, or things the mind can "do".
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">Thought</a> is a mental act that allows humans to make sense of things in the world, and to represent and interpret them in ways that are significant, or which accord with their needs, attachments, goals, commitments, plans, ends, desires, etc. Thinking involves the <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbolic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiotic" title="Semiotic">semiotic</a> mediation of <a href="/wiki/Idea" title="Idea">ideas</a> or data, as when we form <a href="/wiki/Concept" title="Concept">concepts</a>, engage in <a href="/wiki/Problem_solving" title="Problem solving">problem solving</a>, reasoning, and making <a href="/wiki/Choice" title="Choice">decisions</a>. Words that refer to similar concepts and processes include <a href="/wiki/Deliberation" title="Deliberation">deliberation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idea" title="Idea">ideation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">imagination</a>.
</p><p>Thinking is sometimes described as a "higher" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive" title="Cognitive">cognitive</a> function and the analysis of thinking processes is a part of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_psychology" title="Cognitive psychology">cognitive psychology</a>. It is also deeply connected with our capacity to make and use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tools" title="Tools">tools</a>; to understand <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">cause and effect</a>; to recognize patterns of significance; to comprehend and <a href="/wiki/World_disclosure" title="World disclosure">disclose</a> unique contexts of experience or activity; and to respond to the world in a meaningful way.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">Memory</a> is the ability to preserve, retain and subsequently recall knowledge, information, or experience. Although memory has traditionally been a persistent theme in <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries also saw the study of memory emerge as a subject of inquiry within the paradigms of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_psychology" title="Cognitive psychology">cognitive psychology</a>. In recent decades, it has become one of the pillars of a new branch of science called <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_neuroscience" title="Cognitive neuroscience">cognitive neuroscience</a>, a marriage between <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_psychology" title="Cognitive psychology">cognitive psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">Imagination</a> is the activity of generating or evoking novel situations, <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">images</a>, ideas or other <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a> in the mind. It is a characteristically <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjective</a> <i>activity</i>, rather than a direct or passive experience. The term is technically used in <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> for the process of reviving in the mind <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">percepts</a> of objects formerly given in sense perception. Since this use of the term conflicts with that of ordinary <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, some psychologists have preferred to describe this process as "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Imaging" title="wikt:Imaging">imaging</a>" or "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Imagery" title="wikt:Imagery">imagery</a>" or to speak of it as "reproductive" as opposed to "productive" or "constructive" imagination. Things imagined are said to be seen in the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind%27s_eye" title="Mind's eye">mind's eye</a>". Among the many practical functions of imagination are the ability to project possible futures (or histories), to "see" things from another's perspective, and to change the way something is perceived, including to make decisions to respond to, or enact, what is imagined.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">Consciousness</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> (this includes humans) is an aspect of the mind generally thought to comprise qualities such as <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, and the ability to <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perceive</a> the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Personal_identity" title="Personal identity">oneself</a> and one's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a>. It is a subject of much research in <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>. Some philosophers divide <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomena" title="Phenomena">phenomenal</a> consciousness, which is subjective experience itself, and access consciousness, which refers to the global availability of information to processing systems in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bl_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bl-46">[46]</a></sup> Phenomenal consciousness has many different experienced qualities, often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a>. Phenomenal consciousness is usually consciousness <i>of</i> something or <i>about</i> something, a property known as <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a> in philosophy of mind.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Categories_of_mental_phenomena">Categories of mental phenomena</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Categories of mental phenomena">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The mental phenomena brought about by the faculties of the mind have been categorized according to various distinctions. Important distinctions group mental phenomena together according to whether they are <i>sensory</i>, <i>qualitative</i>, <i>propositional</i>, <i>intentional</i>, <i>conscious</i>, <i>occurrent</i> or <i>rational</i>. These different distinctions result in overlapping categorizations. Some mental phenomena, like perception or bodily awareness, are <i>sensory</i>, i.e. based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senses" title="Senses">senses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> These phenomena are of special interest to <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricists</a>, who hold that they are our only source of knowledge about the external world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> They are contrasted with <i>non-sensory</i> phenomena like thoughts or beliefs, which do not involve sense impressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Sensory states are closely related to <i>qualitative states</i>, which have <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a> and are therefore associated with a subjective feeling of what it is like to be in this state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup> Sensory and qualitative states are often contrasted with <i><a href="/wiki/Propositional_attitude" title="Propositional attitude">propositional states</a></i>, which are sometimes said to be non-sensory and non-qualitative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonderichProblems_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-HonderichProblems-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaPhilosophy_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaPhilosophy-22">[22]</a></sup> Propositional states involve attitudes, like <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> or <a href="/wiki/Desire" title="Desire">desire</a>, which a subject has towards a proposition. One problem with this contrast is that some propositional attitudes may have a subjective feeling to them, which would make them qualitative phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tye_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tye-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> This is the case, for example, when actively desiring something. Another problem with this contrast is that some mental phenomena, like perceptions, are both sensory and propositional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Propositional attitudes are <i><a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentional states</a></i>, which have as their characteristic that they refer to or are about objects or states of affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franz_Brentano:_3.2_Intentionality_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Franz_Brentano:_3.2_Intentionality-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crane1998_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Crane1998-30">[30]</a></sup> Some philosophers see intentionality as the mark of the mental, i.e. as what is shared by all and only by mental phenomena. Opponents of this position have argued that there are various mental phenomena, like pains and itches, that lack the representational aspect associated with intentionality and therefore count as non-intentional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crane_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crane-55">[55]</a></sup> This claim is sometimes even extended to all sensory phenomena. It sometimes held that all intentional states are propositional. While this is true for the paradigmatic cases, it has been argued that there is a form of object-directed intentionality, like the fear of snakes, that does not involve propositional attitudes, like the fear that one will be bitten by snakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grzankowski_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grzankowski-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another important distinction among mental states is whether they are <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conscious" title="Conscious">conscious</a></i> or not. Often two types of consciousness are distinguished: <i><a href="/wiki/Consciousness#Types_of_consciousness" title="Consciousness">phenomenal consciousness and access consciousness</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard2018_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard2018-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu2018_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu2018-58">[58]</a></sup> <i>Phenomenal consciousness</i> refers to actual experience. A common view is that some states, like sensations or pains, are necessarily associated with phenomenal consciousness while other states, like beliefs and desires, can be present both with and without phenomenal consciousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> According to some views, conscious mental states are more basic while unconscious states only count as mental if they can arise in phenomenal consciousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaPhilosophy_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaPhilosophy-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bourget_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bourget-23">[23]</a></sup> <i>Access consciousness</i>, on the other hand, refers to mental states that are accessible: they carry information that is available for reasoning and guiding behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard2018_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard2018-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu2018_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu2018-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pernu_16-10"><a href="#cite_note-Pernu-16">[16]</a></sup> This notion is closely related to <i>occurrent mental states</i>, which are not just accessible but also currently active or causally efficacious within the owner's mind. All phenomenally conscious mental states are occurrent but there may also be unconscious occurrent states, like repressed desires, that influence our behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strandberg_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strandberg-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartlett_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frise_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frise-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroeder_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schroeder-65">[65]</a></sup> Occurrent mental states contrast with <i>standing</i> or <i>dispositional mental states</i>, which are part of the subject's mind even though they currently play no role in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartlett_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroeder_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schroeder-65">[65]</a></sup> Mental phenomena are <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rational" title="Rational">rational</a></i> if they are well justified or obey the norms of rationality. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irrational" title="Irrational">Irrational</a> mental phenomena</i>, on the other hand, violate these norms. But not all mental phenomena are rationally evaluable: some are <i>arational</i> and exist outside the domain of rationality. They include urges, dizziness or hunger while beliefs and <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intentions</a> are the paradigmatic examples of rationally evaluable states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nolfi_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nolfi-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mele_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mele-67">[67]</a></sup> Some hold that rationality depends only on structural principles that govern how different mental states should relate to each other while others define rationality in terms of responding correctly to reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Architecture_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Architecture-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mental_contents">Mental contents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mental contents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mental contents are those items that are thought of as being "in" the mind, and capable of being formed and manipulated by mental processes and faculties. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thoughts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concept" title="Concept">concepts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memories" title="Memories">memories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotions</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percept" title="Percept">percepts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intentions</a>. Philosophical theories of mental content include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internalism" title="Internalism">internalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Externalism" title="Externalism">externalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Direct_and_indirect_realism" title="Direct and indirect realism">representationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Memetics" title="Memetics">Memetics</a> is a theory of mental content based on an analogy with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darwinian_evolution" title="Darwinian evolution">Darwinian evolution</a>, which was originated by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hofstadter" title="Douglas Hofstadter">Douglas Hofstadter</a> in the 1980s. It is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_model" title="Evolutionary model">evolutionary model</a> of cultural <a href="/wiki/Information_transfer" title="Information transfer">information transfer</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Meme" title="Meme">meme</a>, analogous to a <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a>, is an idea, belief, pattern of behaviour (etc.) "hosted" in one or more individual minds, and can reproduce itself from mind to mind. Thus what would otherwise be regarded as one individual influencing another to adopt a belief, is seen memetically as a meme reproducing itself.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_the_brain">Relation to the brain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Relation to the brain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_neuroscience" title="Cognitive neuroscience">Cognitive neuroscience</a></div>
<p>In animals, the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>, or <i>encephalon</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_(language)" title="Greek (language)">Greek</a> for "in the head"), is the control center of the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>, responsible for <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thought</a>. In most animals, the brain is located in the head, protected by the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> and close to the primary sensory apparatus of <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">vision</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hearing_(sense)" title="Hearing (sense)">hearing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equilibrioception" title="Equilibrioception">equilibrioception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">olfaction</a>. While all <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> have a brain, most <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> have either a centralized brain or collections of individual <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>. Primitive animals such as <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a> do not have a brain at all. Brains can be extremely complex. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a> contains around 86 billion <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>, each linked to as many as 10,000 others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Understanding the relationship between the brain and the mind –the <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">mind–body problem</a> – is one of the central issues in the history of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, a challenging problem both philosophically and scientifically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> There are three major philosophical schools of thought concerning the answer: dualism, materialism, and idealism. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">Dualism</a> holds that the mind exists independently of the brain;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> holds that mental phenomena are identical to neuronal phenomena;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DicPhil_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-DicPhil-77">[77]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idealism_(philosophy)" title="Idealism (philosophy)">idealism</a> holds that only mental phenomena exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DicPhil_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-DicPhil-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Through most of history many philosophers found it inconceivable that cognition could be implemented by a physical substance such as brain tissue (that is neurons and synapses).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Descartes</a>, who thought extensively about mind-brain relationships, found it possible to explain reflexes and other simple behaviors in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Description_of_the_Human_Body" title="The Description of the Human Body">mechanistic terms</a>, although he did not believe that complex thought, especially language, could be explained by reference to the physical brain alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most straightforward scientific evidence of a strong relationship between the physical brain <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and the mind is the impact physical alterations to the brain have on the mind, such as with <a href="/wiki/Traumatic_brain_injury" title="Traumatic brain injury">traumatic brain injury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive_drug" title="Psychoactive drug">psychoactive drug</a> use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a> notes that drug-mind interaction indicates an intimate connection between the brain and the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the philosophical questions, the relationship between mind and brain involves a number of scientific questions, including understanding the relationship between mental activity and brain activity, the exact mechanisms by which drugs influence <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Neural_correlates_of_consciousness" title="Neural correlates of consciousness">neural correlates of consciousness</a>.
</p><p>Theoretical approaches to explain how mind emerges from the brain include <a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">connectionism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computationalism" title="Computationalism">computationalism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayesian_brain" title="Bayesian brain">Bayesian brain</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_human_intelligence" title="Evolution of human intelligence">evolution of human intelligence</a> refers to several theories that aim to describe how human <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">intelligence</a> has <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> in relation to the evolution of the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_language" title="Origin of language">origin of language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The timeline of <a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">human evolution</a> spans some 7 million years, from the separation of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Pan_(genus)" title="Pan (genus)">Pan</a></i> until the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">behavioral modernity</a> by 50,000 years ago. Of this timeline, the first 3 million years concern <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahelanthropus_tchadensis" title="Sahelanthropus tchadensis">Sahelanthropus</a></i>, the following 2 million concern <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a></i>, while the final 2 million span the history of actual <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_(genus)" title="Homo (genus)">Homo</a></i> species (the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>).
</p><p>Many traits of human intelligence, such as <a href="/wiki/Empathy" title="Empathy">empathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_mind" title="Theory of mind">theory of mind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mourning" title="Mourning">mourning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">ritual</a>, and the use of <a href="/wiki/Great_ape_language" title="Great ape language">symbols</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tool" title="Tool">tools</a>, are already apparent in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_ape" title="Great ape">great apes</a> although in lesser sophistication than in humans.
</p><p>There is a debate between supporters of the idea of a sudden emergence of intelligence, or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioural_modernity#Great_leap_forward" title="Behavioural modernity">Great leap forward</a>" and those of a gradual or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioural_modernity#Continuum_hypothesis" title="Behavioural modernity">continuum hypothesis</a>.
</p><p>Theories of the evolution of intelligence include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Robin_Dunbar" title="Robin Dunbar">Robin Dunbar</a>'s social brain hypothesis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Social_Brain_Hypothesis_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Social_Brain_Hypothesis-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Miller_(psychologist)" title="Geoffrey Miller (psychologist)">Geoffrey Miller</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a> hypothesis concerning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexual_selection_in_human_evolution" title="Sexual selection in human evolution">Sexual selection in human evolution</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li>The ecological dominance-social competition (EDSC)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flinn_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-flinn-85">[85]</a></sup> explained by Mark V. Flinn, David C. Geary and Carol V. Ward based mainly on work by <a href="/wiki/Richard_D._Alexander" title="Richard D. Alexander">Richard D. Alexander</a>.</li>
<li>The idea of intelligence as a signal of good health and resistance to disease.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Group_selection" title="Group selection">Group selection</a> theory contends that organism characteristics that provide benefits to a group (clan, tribe, or larger population) can evolve despite individual disadvantages such as those cited above.</li>
<li>The idea that intelligence is connected with nutrition, and thereby with status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> A higher IQ could be a signal that an individual comes from and lives in a physical and social environment where nutrition levels are high, and vice versa.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">Philosophy of mind</a></div>
<p>Philosophy of mind is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that studies the nature of the mind, <a href="/wiki/Mental_event" title="Mental event">mental events</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_function" title="Mental function">mental functions</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_property" title="Mental property">mental properties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> and their relationship to the physical body. The <i><a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">mind–body problem</a></i>, i.e. the relationship of the mind to the body, is commonly seen as the central issue in philosophy of mind, although there are other issues concerning the nature of the mind that do not involve its relation to the physical body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim1_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim1-87">[87]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Manuel_Rodriguez_Delgado" title="José Manuel Rodriguez Delgado">José Manuel Rodriguez Delgado</a> writes, "In present popular usage, soul and mind are not clearly differentiated and some people, more or less consciously, still feel that the soul, and perhaps the mind, may enter or leave the body as independent entities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">Dualism</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a></i> are the two major schools of thought that attempt to resolve the mind–body problem. Dualism is the position that mind and body are in some way separate from each other. It can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plato_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plato-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rob_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rob-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a> schools of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sa_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sa-93">[93]</a></sup> but it was most precisely formulated by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> in the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-De-94">[94]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">Substance dualists</a></i> argue that the mind is an independently existing substance, whereas <i><a href="/wiki/Property_dualism" title="Property dualism">Property dualists</a></i> maintain that the mind is a group of independent properties that <a href="/wiki/Emergentism" title="Emergentism">emerge</a> from and cannot be reduced to the brain, but that it is not a distinct substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Du_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Du-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 20th century philosopher Martin Heidegger suggested that subjective experience and activity (i.e. the "mind") cannot be made sense of in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Cartesian</a> "substances" that bear "properties" at all (whether the mind itself is thought of as a distinct, separate kind of substance or not). This is because the nature of subjective, <i>qualitative</i> experience is incoherent in terms of – or semantically <a href="/wiki/Commensurability_(philosophy_of_science)" title="Commensurability (philosophy of science)">incommensurable</a> with the concept of – substances that bear properties. This is a fundamentally <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontological</a> argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a>, for example, argues there is no such thing as a narrative center called the "mind", but that instead there is simply a collection of sensory inputs and outputs: different kinds of "software" running in parallel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dennett1991_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett1991-97">[97]</a></sup> Psychologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B.F._Skinner" title="B.F. Skinner">B.F. Skinner</a> argued that the mind is an explanatory fiction that diverts attention from environmental causes of behavior;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> he considered the mind a "black box" and thought that mental processes may be better conceived of as forms of covert verbal behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Philosopher <a href="/wiki/David_Chalmers" title="David Chalmers">David Chalmers</a> has argued that the third person approach to uncovering mind and consciousness is not effective, such as looking into other's brains or observing human conduct, but that a first person approach is necessary.   Such a first person perspective indicates that the mind must be conceptualized as something distinct from the brain.
</p><p>The mind has also been described as manifesting from moment to moment, one thought moment at a time as a fast flowing stream, where sense impressions and mental phenomena are constantly changing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aggregates_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aggregates-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karunamuni2017_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karunamuni2017-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_the_body">Relation to the body</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Relation to the body">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Descartes_mind_and_body.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="609" data-file-width="493" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Descartes_mind_and_body.gif/200px-Descartes_mind_and_body.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Descartes_mind_and_body.gif/300px-Descartes_mind_and_body.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Descartes_mind_and_body.gif/400px-Descartes_mind_and_body.gif 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Descartes_mind_and_body.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>' illustration of <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_dualism" title="Mind–body dualism">mind–body dualism</a>. Descartes believed inputs are passed on by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_organs" title="Sensory organs">Sensory organs</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Pineal_gland" title="Pineal gland">epiphysis</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> and from there to the immaterial spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Descartes1641_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Descartes1641-101">[101]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><i>Monism</i> is the position that mind and body are not <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiologically</a> and ontologically distinct kinds of entities. This view was first advocated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Philosophy" title="Western Philosophy">Western Philosophy</a> by <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> in the 5th Century BC and was later espoused by the 17th Century <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spin_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spin-102">[102]</a></sup> According to Spinoza's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dual-aspect_theory" title="Dual-aspect theory">dual-aspect theory</a>, mind and body are two aspects of an underlying reality which he variously described as "Nature" or "God".
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">Physicalists</a></i> argue that only the entities postulated by physical theory exist, and that the mind will eventually be explained in terms of these entities as physical theory continues to evolve.</li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idealism_(philosophy)" title="Idealism (philosophy)">Idealists</a></i> maintain that the mind is all that exists and that the external world is either mental itself, or an illusion created by the mind.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Neutral_monism" title="Neutral monism">Neutral monists</a></i> adhere to the position that perceived things in the world can be regarded as either physical or mental depending on whether one is interested in their relationship to other things in the world or their relationship to the perceiver. For example, a red spot on a wall is physical in its dependence on the wall and the pigment of which it is made, but it is mental in so far as its perceived redness depends on the workings of the visual system. Unlike dual-aspect theory, neutral monism does not posit a more fundamental substance of which mind and body are aspects.</li></ul>
<p>The most common monisms in the 20th and 21st centuries have all been variations of physicalism; these positions include <a href="/wiki/Behaviorism" title="Behaviorism">behaviorism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Type_physicalism" title="Type physicalism">type identity theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anomalous_monism" title="Anomalous monism">anomalous monism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Functionalism (philosophy of mind)">functionalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many modern philosophers of mind adopt either a <i>reductive</i> or <i>non-reductive physicalist</i> position, maintaining in their different ways that the mind is not something separate from the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kim-103">[103]</a></sup> These approaches have been particularly influential in the sciences, e.g. in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology" title="Sociobiology">sociobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology" title="Evolutionary psychology">evolutionary psychology</a> and the various <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neurosciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PsyBio_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-PsyBio-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeDoux_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeDoux-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RussNor_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-RussNor-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DawkRich_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-DawkRich-107">[107]</a></sup> Other philosophers, however, adopt a non-physicalist position which challenges the notion that the mind is a purely physical construct.
</p>
<ul><li><i>Reductive physicalists</i> assert that all mental states and properties will eventually be explained by scientific accounts of physiological processes and states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pat_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pat-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paul-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smart_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smart-110">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Non-reductive physicalists</i> argue that although the brain is all there <i>is</i> to the mind, the predicates and vocabulary used in mental descriptions and explanations are indispensable, and cannot be reduced to the language and lower-level explanations of physical science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidson_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pu_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pu-112">[112]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Continued progress in <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> has helped to clarify many of these issues, and its findings have been taken by many to support <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalists</a>' assertions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koch_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koch-114">[114]</a></sup> Nevertheless, our knowledge is incomplete, and modern philosophers of mind continue to discuss how subjective <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Intentional_stance" title="Intentional stance">intentional</a> mental states can be <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturally</a> explained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Int_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Int-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searleint_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Searleint-116">[116]</a></sup> There is also the problem of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">Quantum Mechanics</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_interpretation" title="Copenhagen interpretation">Copenhagen interpretation</a> can be understood as a form of <a href="/wiki/Perspectivism" title="Perspectivism">perspectivism</a>. As the Copenhagen interpretation is not universally accepted, an exact mechanism of the <a href="/wiki/Wave_function_collapse" title="Wave function collapse">collapse of the wavefunction</a> remains elusive, and the role of mind in fundamental physical theory is unclear.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_study">Scientific study</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Scientific study">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mind&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>.
